</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Defining the rotation as retrograde, as done by NASA space missions and the USGS, puts <a href="/wiki/Ishtar_Terra" title="Ishtar Terra">Ishtar Terra</a> in the northern hemisphere and makes the axial tilt 2.64°. Following the <a href="/wiki/Right-hand_rule" title="Right-hand rule">right-hand rule</a> for prograde rotation puts Ishtar Terra in the southern hemisphere and makes the axial tilt 177.36°.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Venus</b> is the second <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, orbiting it every 224.7 <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_venus_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_venus-13">[12]</a></sup> It has the longest <a href="/wiki/Rotation_period" title="Rotation period">rotation period</a> (243 days) of any planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and rotates in the <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">opposite direction</a> to most other planets (meaning the Sun would rise in the west and set in the east).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> It does not have any <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>. It is named after the <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Roman goddess of love and beauty</a>. It is the second-brightest natural object in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, reaching an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of −4.6 – bright enough to cast shadows at night and, rarely, visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> in broad daylight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Orbiting within <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">Earth's orbit</a>, Venus is an <a href="/wiki/Inferior_and_superior_planets" title="Inferior and superior planets">inferior planet</a> and never appears to venture far from the Sun; its maximum <a href="/wiki/Angular_distance" title="Angular distance">angular distance</a> from the Sun (<a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongation</a>) is 47.8°.
</p><p>Venus is a <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planet</a> and is sometimes called Earth's "sister planet" because of their similar size, mass, proximity to the Sun, and bulk composition. It is radically different from Earth in other respects. It has the densest <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of the four terrestrial planets, consisting of more than 96% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> at the planet's surface is 92 times that of Earth, or roughly the pressure found 900 m (3,000 ft) underwater on Earth. Venus is by far the hottest planet in the Solar System, with a mean surface temperature of 735 K (462 °C; 863 °F), even though <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> is closer to the Sun. Venus is shrouded by an opaque layer of highly reflective clouds of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, preventing its surface from being seen from space in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>. It may have had water oceans in the past,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> but these would have vaporized as the temperature rose due to a <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">runaway greenhouse effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jakosky_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jakosky-19">[18]</a></sup> The water has probably <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">photodissociated</a>, and the free hydrogen has been <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">swept into interplanetary space</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> because of the lack of a <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">planetary magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solarwind_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-solarwind-20">[19]</a></sup> Venus's surface is a dry desertscape interspersed with slab-like rocks and is periodically resurfaced by <a href="/wiki/Volcanology_of_Venus" title="Volcanology of Venus">volcanism</a>.
</p><p>As one of the brightest objects in the sky, Venus has been a major fixture in human culture for as long as records have existed. It has been made sacred to gods of many cultures, and has been a prime inspiration for writers and poets as the <b>morning star</b> and <b>evening star</b>. Venus was the first planet to have its motions plotted across the sky, as early as the second millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-practice_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-practice-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the closest planet to Earth, Venus has been a prime target for early interplanetary exploration. It was the first planet beyond Earth visited by a spacecraft (<i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_2" title="Mariner 2">Mariner 2</a></i> in 1962), and the first to be successfully landed on (by <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_7" title="Venera 7">Venera 7</a></i> in 1970). Venus's thick clouds render observation of its surface impossible in visible light, and the first detailed maps did not emerge until the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Magellan_(spacecraft)" title="Magellan (spacecraft)">Magellan orbiter</a> in 1991. Plans have been proposed for <a href="/wiki/Rover_(space_exploration)" title="Rover (space exploration)">rovers</a> or more complex missions, but they are hindered by Venus's hostile surface conditions.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="Venus, without its atmosphere, is placed side by side with Earth. They are nearly the same size, though Venus is slightly smaller." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1557" data-file-width="3000" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Venus%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Venus%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Venus%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Venus%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Venus%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Venus%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size comparison with <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></div></div></div>
<p>Venus is one of the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> in the Solar System, meaning that it is a rocky body like Earth. It is similar to Earth in size and mass, and is often described as Earth's "sister" or "twin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> The diameter of Venus is 12,103.6 km (7,520.8 mi)—only 638.4 km (396.7 mi) less than Earth's—and its mass is 81.5% of Earth's. Conditions on the Venusian surface differ radically from those on Earth because its dense <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> is 96.5% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, with most of the remaining 3.5% being <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mapping_of_Venus" title="Mapping of Venus">Mapping of Venus</a></div>
<p>The Venusian surface was a subject of speculation until some of its secrets were revealed by <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a> in the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Venera" title="Venera">Venera</a> landers in 1975 and 1982 returned images of a surface covered in sediment and relatively angular rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> The surface was mapped in detail by <a href="/wiki/Magellan_(spacecraft)" title="Magellan (spacecraft)"><i>Magellan</i></a> in 1990–91. The ground shows evidence of extensive <a href="/wiki/Volcanology_of_Venus" title="Volcanology of Venus">volcanism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> in the atmosphere may indicate that there have been some recent eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 80% of the Venusian surface is covered by smooth, volcanic plains, consisting of 70% plains with wrinkle ridges and 10% smooth or lobate plains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highland_continent" title="Highland continent">highland "continents"</a> make up the rest of its surface area, one lying in the planet's northern hemisphere and the other just south of the equator. The northern continent is called <a href="/wiki/Ishtar_Terra" title="Ishtar Terra">Ishtar Terra</a> after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ishtar" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylonian</a> goddess of love, and is about the size of Australia. <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Montes" title="Maxwell Montes">Maxwell Montes</a>, the highest mountain on Venus, lies on Ishtar Terra. Its peak is 11 km (7 mi) above the Venusian average surface elevation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetology_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetology-28">[27]</a></sup> The southern continent is called <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite_Terra" title="Aphrodite Terra">Aphrodite Terra</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> goddess of love, and is the larger of the two highland regions at roughly the size of South America. A network of fractures and faults covers much of this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaufmann_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufmann-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The absence of evidence of <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a> flow accompanying any of the visible <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">calderas</a> remains an enigma. The planet has few <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a>, demonstrating that the surface is relatively young, approximately 300–600 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo98_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo98-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom1994_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strom1994-31">[30]</a></sup> Venus has some unique surface features in addition to the impact craters, mountains, and valleys commonly found on rocky planets. Among these are flat-topped volcanic features called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farra_(Venus)" title="Farra (Venus)">farra</a>", which look somewhat like pancakes and range in size from 20 to 50 km (12 to 31 mi) across, and from 100 to 1,000 m (330 to 3,280 ft) high; radial, star-like fracture systems called "novae"; features with both radial and concentric fractures resembling spider webs, known as "<a href="/wiki/Arachnoid_(astrogeology)" title="Arachnoid (astrogeology)">arachnoids</a>"; and "coronae", circular rings of fractures sometimes surrounded by a depression. These features are volcanic in origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frankel_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frankel-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Venusian surface features are named after historical and mythological women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Exceptions are Maxwell Montes, named after <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a>, and highland regions <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Regio" title="Alpha Regio">Alpha Regio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta_Regio" title="Beta Regio">Beta Regio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ovda_Regio" title="Ovda Regio">Ovda Regio</a>. The latter three features were named before the current system was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>, the body which oversees <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nomenclature" title="Planetary nomenclature">planetary nomenclature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl-magellan_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl-magellan-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The longitudes of physical features on Venus are expressed relative to its <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian" title="Prime meridian">prime meridian</a>. The original prime meridian passed through the radar-bright spot at the centre of the oval feature Eve, located south of Alpha Regio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_1994_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies_1994-35">[34]</a></sup> After the Venera missions were completed, the prime meridian was redefined to pass through the central peak in the crater Ariadne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl-magellan2_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl-magellan2-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_geology">Surface geology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Venus" title="Geology of Venus">Geology of Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcanology_of_Venus" title="Volcanology of Venus">Volcanology of Venus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg"><img alt="Image is false-colour, with Maat Mons represented in hues of gold and fiery red, against a black background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="5000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg/220px-Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg/330px-Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg/440px-Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maat_Mons_on_Venus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-colour image of <a href="/wiki/Maat_Mons" title="Maat Mons">Maat Mons</a> with a vertical exaggeration of 22.5</div></div></div>
<p>Much of the Venusian surface appears to have been shaped by volcanic activity. Venus has several times as many volcanoes as Earth, and it has 167 large volcanoes that are over 100 km (62 mi) across. The only volcanic complex of this size on Earth is the <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">Big Island</a> of Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frankel_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frankel-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>154</span></sup> This is not because Venus is more volcanically active than Earth, but because its crust is older. Earth's <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> is continually recycled by <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> at the boundaries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plates</a>, and has an average age of about 100 million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> whereas the Venusian surface is estimated to be 300–600 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo98_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo98-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frankel_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Frankel-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several lines of evidence point to ongoing <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> activity on Venus. During the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Venera" title="Venera">Venera</a> program, the <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_9" title="Venera 9">Venera 9</a></i> orbiter obtained spectroscopic evidence of <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> on Venus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_12" title="Venera 12">Venera 12</a></i> descent probe obtained additional evidence of lightning and <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell,_Philips_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell,_Philips-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">Venus Express</a></i> in 2007 detected <a href="/wiki/Whistler_(radio)" title="Whistler (radio)">whistler waves</a> further confirming the occurrence of lightning on Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_2007_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_2007-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venus_Express_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venus_Express-43">[42]</a></sup> One possibility is that ash from a volcanic eruption was generating the lightning. Another piece of evidence comes from measurements of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> concentrations in the atmosphere, which dropped by a factor of 10 between 1978 and 1986, jumped in 2006, and again declined 10-fold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_2012-12-03_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA_2012-12-03-44">[43]</a></sup> This may mean that levels had been boosted several times by large volcanic eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcq2012_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcq2012-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008 and 2009, the first direct evidence for ongoing volcanism was observed by <i>Venus Express</i>, in the form of four transient localized infrared hot spots within the rift zone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganiki_Chasma" title="Ganiki Chasma">Ganis Chasma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_Ganis_Chasma_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS_Ganis_Chasma-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[n 1]</a></sup> near the shield volcano <a href="/wiki/Maat_Mons" title="Maat Mons">Maat Mons</a>. Three of the spots were observed in more than one successive orbit. These spots are thought to represent lava freshly released by volcanic eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2015_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2015-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_2015-06-18_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA_2015-06-18-50">[48]</a></sup> The actual temperatures are not known, because the size of the hot spots could not be measured, but are likely to have been in the 800–1,100 K (527–827 °C; 980–1,520 °F) range, relative to a normal temperature of 740 K (467 °C; 872 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shalygin2015_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shalygin2015-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg"><img alt="The plains of Venus are outlined in red and gold, with impact craters leaving golden rings across the surface" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="5000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg/220px-PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg/330px-PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg/440px-PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA00103_Venus_-_3-D_Perspective_View_of_Lavinia_Planitia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">Impact craters</a> on the surface of Venus (false-colour image reconstructed from radar data)</div></div></div>
<p>Almost a thousand impact craters on Venus are evenly distributed across its surface. On other cratered bodies, such as Earth and the Moon, craters show a range of states of degradation. On the Moon, degradation is caused by subsequent impacts, whereas on Earth it is caused by wind and rain erosion. On Venus, about 85% of the craters are in pristine condition. The number of craters, together with their well-preserved condition, indicates the planet underwent a global resurfacing event about 300–600 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo98_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo98-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom1994_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strom1994-31">[30]</a></sup> followed by a decay in volcanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> Whereas Earth's crust is in continuous motion, Venus is thought to be unable to sustain such a process. Without plate tectonics to dissipate heat from its mantle, Venus instead undergoes a cyclical process in which mantle temperatures rise until they reach a critical level that weakens the crust. Then, over a period of about 100 million years, subduction occurs on an enormous scale, completely recycling the crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frankel_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Frankel-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Venusian craters range from 3 to 280 km (2 to 174 mi) in diameter. No craters are smaller than 3 km, because of the effects of the dense atmosphere on incoming objects. Objects with less than a certain <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> are slowed down so much by the atmosphere that they do not create an impact crater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> Incoming projectiles less than 50 m (160 ft) in diameter will fragment and burn up in the atmosphere before reaching the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_structure.jpg"><img alt="Venus is represented without its atmosphere; the mantle (red) is slightly larger than the core (yellow)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Venus_structure.jpg/220px-Venus_structure.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Venus_structure.jpg/330px-Venus_structure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Venus_structure.jpg/440px-Venus_structure.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_structure.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The internal structure of Venus – the crust (outer layer), the mantle (middle layer) and the core (yellow inner layer)</div></div></div>
<p>Without seismic data or knowledge of its <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia" title="Moment of inertia">moment of inertia</a>, little direct information is available about the internal structure and <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemistry</a> of Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goettel_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-goettel-55">[53]</a></sup> The similarity in size and density between Venus and Earth suggests they share a similar internal structure: a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a>. Like that of Earth, the Venusian core is at least partially liquid because the two planets have been cooling at about the same rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> The slightly smaller size of Venus means pressures are 24% lower in its deep interior than Earth's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> The principal difference between the two planets is the lack of evidence for <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> on Venus, possibly because its crust is too strong to <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduct</a> without water to make it less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscous" title="Viscous">viscous</a>. This results in reduced heat loss from the planet, preventing it from cooling and providing a likely explanation for its lack of an internally generated <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
Instead, Venus may lose its internal heat in periodic major resurfacing events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo98_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo98-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere_and_climate">Atmosphere and climate</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright" style="background-color:light grey"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px;background-color:light grey"><div class="tsingle" style="background-color:light grey;margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg"><img alt="The atmosphere of Venus appears darker and lined with shadows. The shadows trace the prevailing wind direction." data-file-height="2158" data-file-width="2008" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/230px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/345px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/460px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Cloud structure in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">the Venusian atmosphere</a> in 1979, revealed by observations in the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> band by <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_Orbiter" title="Pioneer Venus Orbiter">Pioneer Venus Orbiter</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="background-color:light grey;margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_globe.jpg"><img alt="A false-colour image of Venus: ribbons of lighter colour stretch haphazardly across the surface. Plainer areas of more even colouration lie between." data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="4096" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Venus_globe.jpg/230px-Venus_globe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Venus_globe.jpg/345px-Venus_globe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Venus_globe.jpg/460px-Venus_globe.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Global <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> view of Venus (without the clouds) from <a href="/wiki/Magellan_(spacecraft)" title="Magellan (spacecraft)"><i>Magellan</i></a> between 1990 and 1994</div></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Atmosphere of Venus</a></div>
<p>Venus has an extremely dense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmosphere</a> composed of 96.5% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, 3.5% <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and traces of other gases, most notably <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SolarSystemEncyclopedia_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-SolarSystemEncyclopedia-59">[57]</a></sup> The mass of its atmosphere is 93 times that of Earth's, whereas the pressure at its surface is about 92 times that at Earth's—a pressure equivalent to that at a depth of nearly 1 kilometre (0.62 mi) under Earth's oceans. The density at the surface is 65 kg/m<sup>3</sup>, 6.5% that of water or 50 times as dense as Earth's atmosphere at 293 K (20 °C; 68 °F) at sea level. The CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>-rich atmosphere generates the strongest <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> in the Solar System, creating surface temperatures of at least 735 K (462 °C; 864 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_venus_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_venus-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> This makes Venus's surface hotter than <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>'s, which has a minimum surface temperature of 53 K (−220 °C; −364 °F) and maximum surface temperature of 700 K (427 °C; 801 °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> even though Venus is nearly twice Mercury's distance from the Sun and thus receives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law_of_radiation" title="Inverse square law of radiation">only 25%</a> of Mercury's solar <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">irradiance</a>. This temperature is higher than that used for <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilization</a>.
</p><p>Studies have suggested that billions of years ago Venus's atmosphere was much more like Earth's than it is now, and that there may have been substantial quantities of liquid water on the surface, but after a period of 600 million to several billion years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas39_540_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-baas39_540-63">[61]</a></sup> a runaway greenhouse effect was caused by the evaporation of that original water, which generated a critical level of greenhouse gases in its atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasting_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasting-64">[62]</a></sup> Although the surface conditions on Venus are no longer hospitable to any Earthlike life that may have formed before this event, there is speculation on the possibility that life exists in the  upper cloud layers of Venus, 50 km (31 mi) up from the surface, where the temperature ranges between 303 and 353 K (30 and 80 °C; 86 and 176 °F) but the environment is acidic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cockell1999_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cockell1999-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_inertia" title="Thermal inertia">Thermal inertia</a> and the transfer of heat by winds in the lower atmosphere mean that the temperature of Venus's surface does not vary significantly between the night and day sides, despite Venus's extremely slow rotation. Winds at the surface are slow, moving at a few kilometres per hour, but because of the high density of the atmosphere at the surface, they exert a significant amount of force against obstructions, and transport dust and small stones across the surface. This alone would make it difficult for a human to walk through, even if the heat, pressure, and lack of oxygen were not a problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Above the dense CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span> layer are thick clouds consisting mainly of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, which is formed by <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> and water through a chemical reaction resulting in sulfuric acid hydrate. Additionally, the atmosphere consists of approximately 1% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferric_chloride" title="Ferric chloride">ferric chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kras006_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-kras006-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> Other possible constituents of the cloud particles are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferric_sulfate" title="Ferric sulfate">ferric sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphoric_anhydride" title="Phosphoric anhydride">phosphoric anhydride</a>. Clouds at different levels have different compositions and particle size distributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kras006_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-kras006-69">[67]</a></sup> These clouds reflect and scatter about 90% of the sunlight that falls on them back into space, and prevent visual observation of Venus's surface. The permanent cloud cover means that although Venus is closer than Earth to the Sun, it receives less sunlight on the ground. Strong 300 km/h (185 mph) winds at the cloud tops go around Venus about every four to five Earth days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> Winds on Venus move at up to 60 times the speed of its rotation, whereas Earth's fastest winds are only 10–20% rotation speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science328_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-science328-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The surface of Venus is effectively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isothermal" title="Isothermal">isothermal</a>; it retains a constant temperature not only between day and night sides but between the equator and the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-fact-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> Venus's minute <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a>—less than 3°, compared to 23° on Earth—also minimises seasonal temperature variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> The only appreciable variation in temperature occurs with altitude. The highest point on Venus, <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Montes" title="Maxwell Montes">Maxwell Montes</a>, is therefore the coolest point on Venus, with a temperature of about 655 K (380 °C; 715 °F) and an atmospheric pressure of about 4.5 MPa (45 bar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basilevsky_2003_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basilevsky_2003-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGill_2010_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGill_2010-76">[74]</a></sup> In 1995, the <a href="/wiki/Magellan_(spacecraft)" title="Magellan (spacecraft)"><i>Magellan</i> spacecraft</a> imaged a <a href="/wiki/Venus_snow" title="Venus snow">highly reflective substance</a> at the tops of the highest mountain peaks that bore a strong resemblance to terrestrial snow. This substance likely formed from a similar process to snow, albeit at a far higher temperature. Too volatile to condense on the surface, it rose in gaseous form to higher elevations, where it is cooler and could precipitate. The identity of this substance is not known with certainty, but speculation has ranged from elemental <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> to lead sulfide (<a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The clouds of Venus may be capable of producing <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Upadhyay,_H._O._99–108_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Upadhyay,_H._O._99–108-78">[76]</a></sup> The existence of lightning in the atmosphere of Venus has been controversial since the first suspected bursts were detected by the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Venera" title="Venera">Venera probes</a>. In 2006–07, <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">Venus Express</a></i> clearly detected <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_electron_wave" title="Electromagnetic electron wave">whistler mode waves</a>, the signatures of lightning. Their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermittent" title="Intermittent">intermittent</a> appearance indicates a pattern associated with weather activity. According to these measurements, the lightning rate is at least half of that on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_2007_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_2007-42">[41]</a></sup> In 2007, <i>Venus Express</i> discovered that a huge double <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">atmospheric vortex</a> exists at the south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Venus Express</i> also discovered, in 2011, that an <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> layer exists high in the atmosphere of Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esaozone_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-esaozone-81">[79]</a></sup> On 29 January 2013, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a> scientists reported that the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> of Venus streams outwards in a manner similar to "the ion tail seen streaming from a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> under similar conditions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-20130129_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-20130129-82">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20130130_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20130130-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2015 and to a lesser extent in April and May 2016, researchers working on Japan's <i>Akatsuki</i> mission observed bow shapes in the atmosphere of Venus. This was considered direct evidence of the existence of perhaps the largest stationary <a href="/wiki/Gravity_wave" title="Gravity wave">gravity waves</a> in the solar system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:788px;max-width:788px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Atmospheric composition</div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:382px;max-width:382px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif"><img alt="The atmosphere of Earth is represented as a series of coloured spikes. The green of water dominates, while the red of carbon dioxide clusters near the left side." data-file-height="380" data-file-width="898" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif/380px-Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif/570px-Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif/760px-Synthetic_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif 2x" width="380"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Absorption spectrum of a simple gas mixture corresponding to <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif"><img alt="The atmosphere of Venus is represented on the same graph. Here the red of carbon dioxide is almost overwhelming, but the green of water and the purple of carbon monoxide are present." data-file-height="391" data-file-width="972" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif/400px-Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif/600px-Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif/800px-Synthetic_Venus_atmosphere_absorption_spectrum.gif 2x" width="400"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The composition of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">atmosphere of Venus</a> based on <a href="/wiki/HITRAN" title="HITRAN">HITRAN</a> data<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HITRAN_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-HITRAN-87">[85]</a></sup> created using HITRAN on the Web system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hitraniaoru_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-hitraniaoru-88">[86]</a></sup></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Green colour – water vapour, red – carbon dioxide, WN – <a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">wavenumber</a> (other colours have different meanings, lower <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> on the right, higher on the left).</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field_and_core">Magnetic field and core</span></h3>
<p>In 1967, <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_4" title="Venera 4">Venera 4</a></i> found Venus's <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> to be much weaker than that of Earth. This magnetic field is induced by an interaction between the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> rather than by an internal <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">dynamo</a> as in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>. Venus's small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Venus" title="Magnetosphere of Venus">induced magnetosphere</a> provides negligible protection to the atmosphere against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a>.
</p><p>The lack of an intrinsic magnetic field at Venus was surprising, given that it is similar to Earth in size, and was expected also to contain a dynamo at its core. A dynamo requires three things: a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conducting</a> liquid, rotation, and <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>. The core is thought to be electrically conductive and, although its rotation is often thought to be too slow, simulations show it is adequate to produce a dynamo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> This implies that the dynamo is missing because of a lack of convection in Venus's core. On Earth, convection occurs in the liquid outer layer of the core because the bottom of the liquid layer is much hotter than the top. On Venus, a global resurfacing event may have shut down plate tectonics and led to a reduced heat flux through the crust. This caused the mantle temperature to increase, thereby reducing the heat flux out of the core. As a result, no internal geodynamo is available to drive a magnetic field. Instead, the heat from the core is being used to reheat the crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nimmo02_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-nimmo02-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>One possibility is that Venus has no solid inner core,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> or that its core is not cooling, so that the entire liquid part of the core is at approximately the same temperature. Another possibility is that its core has already completely solidified. The state of the core is highly dependent on the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, which is unknown at present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nimmo02_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-nimmo02-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The weak magnetosphere around Venus means that the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> is interacting directly with its outer atmosphere. Here, ions of hydrogen and oxygen are being created by the dissociation of neutral molecules from ultraviolet radiation. The solar wind then supplies energy that gives some of these ions sufficient velocity to escape Venus's gravity field. This erosion process results in a steady loss of low-mass hydrogen, helium, and oxygen ions, whereas higher-mass molecules, such as carbon dioxide, are more likely to be retained. Atmospheric erosion by the solar wind probably led to the loss of most of Venus's water during the first billion years after it formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature450_7170_629_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature450_7170_629-95">[93]</a></sup> The erosion has increased the ratio of higher-mass <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> to lower-mass hydrogen in the atmosphere 100 times compared to the rest of the solar system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_Venus" title="Orbit of Venus">Orbit of Venus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venusorbitsolarsystem.gif"><img alt="the orbits of Mercury, Venus, Earth and Mars are seen in motion from the top down against a spiderweb graph. Earth's orbit leaves a blue trail, while Venus's orbit leaves a yellow trail" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="240" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Venusorbitsolarsystem.gif/220px-Venusorbitsolarsystem.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Venusorbitsolarsystem.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venusorbitsolarsystem.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Venus orbits the Sun at an average distance of about 108 million kilometres (about 0.7 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_Unit" title="Astronomical Unit">AU</a>) and completes an orbit every 224.7 days. Venus is the second planet from the Sun and orbits the Sun approximately 1.6 times (yellow trail) in Earth's 365 days (blue trail)</div></div></div>
<p>Venus orbits the Sun at an average distance of about 0.72 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (108 million <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a>; 67 million <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">mi</a>), and completes an orbit every 224.7 days. Although all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_orbit" title="Planetary orbit">planetary orbits</a> are <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">elliptical</a>, Venus's orbit is the closest to <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circular</a>, with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eccentricity_(orbit)" title="Eccentricity (orbit)">eccentricity</a> of less than 0.01.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-fact-2">[2]</a></sup> When Venus lies between Earth and the Sun in <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy)#Superior_and_inferior" title="Conjunction (astronomy)">inferior conjunction</a>, it makes the closest approach to Earth of any planet at an average distance of 41 million km (25 million mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-fact-2">[2]</a></sup> The planet reaches inferior conjunction every 584 days, on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-fact-2">[2]</a></sup> Because of the <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles#Orbital_shape_(eccentricity)" title="Milankovitch cycles">decreasing eccentricity of Earth's orbit</a>, the minimum distances will become greater over tens of thousands of years. From the year 1 to 5383, there are 526 approaches less than 40 million km; then there are none for about 60,158 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>All the planets in the Solar System orbit the Sun in a counterclockwise direction as viewed from above Earth's north pole. Most planets also rotate on their axes in an anti-clockwise direction, but Venus rotates clockwise in <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">retrograde rotation</a> once every 243 Earth days—the slowest rotation of any planet. Because its rotation is so slow, Venus is very close to spherical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venus_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venus-98">[96]</a></sup> A Venusian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">sidereal day</a> thus lasts longer than a Venusian year (243 versus 224.7 Earth days). Venus's equator rotates at 6.52 km/h (4.05 mph), whereas Earth's rotates at 1,669.8 km/h (1,037.6 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Venus's rotation has slowed down in the 16 years between the <i>Magellan</i> spacecraft and <i>Venus Express</i> visits; each Venusian sidereal day has increased by 6.5 minutes in that time span.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slowing_spin_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-slowing_spin-100">[98]</a></sup> Because of the retrograde rotation, the length of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_day" title="Solar day">solar day</a> on Venus is significantly shorter than the sidereal day, at 116.75 Earth days (making the Venusian solar day shorter than <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>'s 176 Earth days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary-facts_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary-facts-101">[99]</a></sup> One Venusian year is about 1.92 Venusian solar days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compare_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-compare-102">[100]</a></sup> To an observer on the surface of Venus, the Sun would rise in the west and set in the east,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compare_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-compare-102">[100]</a></sup> although Venus's opaque clouds prevent observing the Sun from the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Venus may have formed from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> with a different rotation period and obliquity, reaching its current state because of chaotic spin changes caused by planetary perturbations and <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tidal</a> effects on its dense atmosphere, a change that would have occurred over the course of billions of years. The rotation period of Venus may represent an equilibrium state between tidal locking to the Sun's gravitation, which tends to slow rotation, and an atmospheric tide created by solar heating of the thick Venusian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>
The 584-day average interval between successive close approaches to Earth is almost exactly equal to 5 Venusian solar days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> but the hypothesis of a spin–orbit resonance with Earth has been discounted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj2_230_L123_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj2_230_L123-107">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Venus has no natural satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus202_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus202-108">[106]</a></sup> It has several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid">trojan asteroids</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">quasi-satellite</a> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2002_VE68" title="2002 VE68">2002 VE<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">68</span></span></a></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> and two other temporary trojans, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(322756)_2001_CK32" title="(322756) 2001 CK32">2001 CK<sub>32</sub></a></span> and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2012_XE133" title="2012 XE133">2012 XE<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">133</span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dynamics_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-dynamics-111">[109]</a></sup> In the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Cassini</a> reported a moon orbiting Venus, which was named <a href="/wiki/Neith_(hypothetical_moon)" title="Neith (hypothetical moon)">Neith</a> and numerous sightings were reported over the following <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009631152000000000♠</span>200 years</span>, but most were determined to be stars in the vicinity. Alex Alemi's and <a href="/wiki/David_J._Stevenson" title="David J. Stevenson">David Stevenson</a>'s 2006 study of models of the early Solar System at the <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a> shows Venus likely had at least one moon created by a huge <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact event</a> billions of years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> About 10 million years later, according to the study, another impact reversed the planet's spin direction and caused the Venusian moon gradually to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_deceleration" title="Tidal deceleration">spiral inward</a> until it collided with Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> If later impacts created moons, these were removed in the same way. An alternative explanation for the lack of satellites is the effect of strong solar tides, which can destabilize large satellites orbiting the inner terrestrial planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus202_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-icarus202-108">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg"><img alt="A photograph of the night sky taken from the seashore. A glimmer of sunlight is on the horizon. There are many stars visible. Venus is at the centre, much brighter than any of the stars, and its light can be seen reflected in the ocean." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="725" data-file-width="1161" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg/220px-Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg/330px-Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg/440px-Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus-pacific-levelled.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Venus is always brighter than all other planets or stars as seen from Earth. The second brightest object on the image is Jupiter.</div></div></div>
<p>To the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, Venus appears as a white point of light brighter than any other planet or star (apart from the Sun).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> The planet's mean <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> is -4.14 with a standard deviation of 0.31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-12">[11]</a></sup> The brightest magnitude occurs during crescent phase about one month before or after inferior conjunction. Venus fades to about magnitude −3 when it is backlit by the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MallamaSky_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-MallamaSky-115">[113]</a></sup> The planet is bright enough to be seen in a clear midday sky<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> and is more easily visible when the Sun is low on the horizon or setting. As an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_planet" title="Inferior planet">inferior planet</a>, it always lies within about 47° of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ephemeris_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-ephemeris-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Venus "overtakes" Earth every 584 days as it orbits the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-fact-2">[2]</a></sup> As it does so, it changes from the "Evening Star", visible after sunset, to the "Morning Star", visible before sunrise. Although <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, the other inferior planet, reaches a maximum <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongation</a> of only 28° and is often difficult to discern in twilight, Venus is hard to miss when it is at its brightest. Its greater maximum elongation means it is visible in dark skies long after sunset. As the brightest point-like object in the sky, Venus is a commonly misreported "<a href="/wiki/Unidentified_flying_object" title="Unidentified flying object">unidentified flying object</a>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases">Phases</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">Phases of Venus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phases_Venus.jpg"><img alt="diagram illustrating the phases of Venus, going from full to new, showing that its diameter increases as its visible area decreases" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Phases_Venus.jpg/220px-Phases_Venus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Phases_Venus.jpg/330px-Phases_Venus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Phases_Venus.jpg/440px-Phases_Venus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phases_Venus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The phases of Venus and evolution of its apparent diameter</div></div></div>
<p>As it orbits the Sun, Venus displays <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">phases</a> like those of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">telescopic</a> view. The planet appears as a small and "full" disc when it is on the opposite side of the Sun (at superior <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy)" title="Conjunction (astronomy)">conjunction</a>). Venus shows a larger disc and "quarter phase" at its maximum <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongations</a> from the Sun, and appears its brightest in the night sky. The planet presents a much larger thin "crescent" in telescopic views as it passes along the near side between Earth and the Sun. Venus displays its largest size and "new phase" when it is between Earth and the Sun (at inferior conjunction). Its atmosphere is visible through telescopes by the halo of sunlight refracted around it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ephemeris_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-ephemeris-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transits">Transits</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">Transit of Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2012" title="Transit of Venus, 2012">Transit of Venus, 2012</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg"><img alt="Venus appears as a black bubble on the edge of the Sun's disk, dimmed through filters to a dull orange." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1406" data-file-width="1375" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg/170px-Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg/255px-Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg/340px-Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2004" title="Transit of Venus, 2004">2004 transit of Venus</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Venusian orbit is slightly inclined relative to Earth's orbit; thus, when the planet passes between Earth and the Sun, it usually does not cross the face of the Sun. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transits_of_Venus" title="Transits of Venus">Transits of Venus</a> occur when the planet's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_conjunction" title="Inferior conjunction">inferior conjunction</a> coincides with its presence in the plane of Earth's orbit. Transits of Venus occur in cycles of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009766849680000000♠</span>243 years</span> with the current pattern of transits being pairs of transits separated by eight years, at intervals of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009332932680000000♠</span>105.5 years</span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7009383424840000000♠</span>121.5 years</span>—a pattern first discovered in 1639 by the English astronomer <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Horrocks" title="Jeremiah Horrocks">Jeremiah Horrocks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCLAN_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCLAN-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The latest pair was <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2004" title="Transit of Venus, 2004">June 8, 2004</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2012" title="Transit of Venus, 2012">June 5–6, 2012</a>. The transit could be watched live from many online outlets or observed locally with the right equipment and conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The preceding pair of transits occurred in <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1874" title="Transit of Venus, 1874">December 1874</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1882" title="Transit of Venus, 1882">December 1882</a>; the following pair will occur in December 2117 and December 2125.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> The oldest film known is the 1874 <i><a href="/wiki/Passage_de_Venus" title="Passage de Venus">Passage de Venus</a></i>, showing the 1874 Venus transit of the sun. Historically, transits of Venus were important, because they allowed astronomers to determine the size of the <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a>, and hence the size of the Solar System <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1639" title="Transit of Venus, 1639">as shown by Horrocks in 1639</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_Cook" title="Captain Cook">Captain Cook</a>'s exploration of the east coast of Australia came after he had sailed to <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a> in 1768 to observe a transit of Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pentagram_of_Venus">Pentagram of Venus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_(pentagram).svg"><img alt="The image resembles a complex, spirograph floral pattern with five loops encircling the middle." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="602" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_%28pentagram%29.svg/170px-Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_%28pentagram%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_%28pentagram%29.svg/255px-Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_%28pentagram%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_%28pentagram%29.svg/340px-Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_%28pentagram%29.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_geocentric_orbit_curve_simplified_Line_(pentagram).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pentagram of Venus. Earth is positioned at the centre of the diagram, and the curve represents the direction and distance of Venus as a function of time.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentagram_of_Venus" title="Pentagram of Venus">pentagram of Venus</a> is the path that Venus makes as observed from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Successive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_conjunction" title="Inferior conjunction">inferior conjunctions</a> of Venus repeat very near a 13:8 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> (Earth orbits 8 times for every 13 orbits of Venus), shifting 144° upon sequential inferior conjunctions. The resonance 13:8 ratio is approximate. 8/13 is approximately 0.615385 while Venus orbits the Sun in 0.615187 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Daylight_apparitions">Daylight apparitions</span></h3>
<p>Naked eye observations of Venus during daylight hours exist in several <a href="/wiki/Anecdote" title="Anecdote">anecdotes</a> and records. Astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_Halley" title="Edmund Halley">Edmund Halley</a> calculated its maximum naked eye brightness in 1716, when many Londoners were alarmed by its appearance in the daytime. French emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> once witnessed a daytime apparition of the planet while at a reception in <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup> Another historical daytime observation of the planet took place during the inauguration of the American president <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, on 4 March 1865.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> Although naked eye visibility of Venus's phases is disputed, records exist of observations of its crescent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ashen_light">Ashen light</span></h3>
<p>A long-standing mystery of Venus observations is the so-called <a href="/wiki/Ashen_light" title="Ashen light">ashen light</a>—an apparent weak illumination of its dark side, seen when the planet is in the crescent phase. The first claimed observation of ashen light was made in 1643, but the existence of the illumination has never been reliably confirmed. Observers have speculated it may result from electrical activity in the Venusian atmosphere, but it could be illusory, resulting from the physiological effect of observing a bright, crescent-shaped object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell,_Philips_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell,_Philips-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Studies">Studies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_studies">Early studies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Drawing.jpg"><img alt="a hand-drawn sequence of images showing Venus passing over the edge of the Sun's disk, leaving an illusory drop of shadow behind" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="899" data-file-width="734" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Venus_Drawing.jpg/220px-Venus_Drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Venus_Drawing.jpg/330px-Venus_Drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Venus_Drawing.jpg/440px-Venus_Drawing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "<a href="/wiki/Black_drop_effect" title="Black drop effect">black drop effect</a>" as recorded during the 1769 transit</div></div></div>
<p>Venus was known to ancient civilizations both as the "morning star" and as the "evening star", names that reflect the early assumption that these were two separate objects. The ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a>, who recognized Venus as a single object,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooley_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooley-129">[127]</a></sup> believed that it was their goddess <a href="/wiki/Inanna" title="Inanna">Inanna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooley_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooley-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> <span id="Inanna_and_Shukaletuda"></span>Inanna's movements in several of her myths, including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inanna_and_Shukaletuda" title="Inanna and Shukaletuda">Inanna and Shukaletuda</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Inanna#Descent_into_the_Underworld" title="Inanna">Inanna's Descent into the Underworld</a></i> appear to parallel the motion of the planet Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooley_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cooley-129">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Venus_tablet_of_Ammisaduqa" title="Venus tablet of Ammisaduqa">Venus tablet of Ammisaduqa</a>, believed to have been compiled around the mid-seventeenth century BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> shows the Babylonians understood the two were a single object, referred to in the tablet as the "bright queen of the sky", and could support this view with detailed observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancient Greeks thought that Venus was two separate stars: <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_(morning_star)" title="Phosphorus (morning star)">Phosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hesperus" title="Hesperus">Hesperus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> credited the realization that they were a single object to <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> in the sixth century BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_Laertius" title="Diogenes Laertius">Diogenes Laertius</a> argued that <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> was probably responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> The Chinese historically referred to the morning Venus as "the Great White" (<i>Tài-bái</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">太白</span>) or "the Opener (Starter) of Brightness" (<i>Qǐ-míng</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">啟明</span>), and the evening Venus as "the Excellent West One" (<i>Cháng-gēng</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">長庚</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> The Romans designated the morning aspect of Venus as <a href="/wiki/Lucifer" title="Lucifer">Lucifer</a>, literally "Light-Bringer", and the evening aspect as <a href="/wiki/Hesperus" title="Hesperus">Vesper</a>, both literal translations of the respective Greek names.
</p><p>In the second century, in his astronomical treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> theorized that both Mercury and Venus are located between the Sun and the Earth. The 11th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">Persian astronomer</a> <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> claimed to have observed the <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">transit of Venus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-137">[135]</a></sup> which later astronomers took as confirmation of Ptolemy's theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> In the 12th century, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Bajjah" title="Ibn Bajjah">Ibn Bajjah</a> observed "two planets as black spots on the face of the Sun"; these were later identified as the transits of Venus and Mercury by the <a href="/wiki/Maragheh_observatory" title="Maragheh observatory">Maragha</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qotb_al-Din_Shirazi" title="Qotb al-Din Shirazi">Qotb al-Din Shirazi</a> in the 13th century, though this identification cannot be true as there were no Venus transits in Ibn Bajjah's lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[n 2]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phases-of-Venus.svg"><img alt="Venus is shown in various positions in its orbit round the Sun, with each position marking a different amount of surface illumination" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/220px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/330px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/440px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phases-of-Venus.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a>'s discovery that Venus showed phases (although remaining near the Sun in Earth's sky) proved that it orbits the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and not <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></div></div></div>
<p>When the Italian physicist <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> first observed the planet in the early 17th century, he found it showed <a href="/wiki/Planetary_phase" title="Planetary phase">phases</a> like the Moon, varying from crescent to gibbous to full and vice versa. When Venus is furthest from the Sun in the sky, it shows a <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">half-lit phase</a>, and when it is closest to the Sun in the sky, it shows as a crescent or full phase. This could be possible only if Venus orbited the Sun, and this was among the first observations to clearly contradict the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic</a> <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">geocentric model</a> that the Solar System was concentric and centred on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmieri_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmieri-143">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fegley_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fegley-144">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1639" title="Transit of Venus, 1639">1639 transit of Venus</a> was accurately predicted by <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Horrocks" title="Jeremiah Horrocks">Jeremiah Horrocks</a> and observed by him and his friend, <a href="/wiki/William_Crabtree" title="William Crabtree">William Crabtree</a>, at each of their respective homes, on 4 December 1639 (24 November under the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a> in use at that time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kollerstrom_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kollerstrom-145">[142]</a></sup>
