</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">atmosphere of Venus</a> was discovered in 1761 by Russian polymath <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Mikhail Lomonosov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marov2004_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marov2004-146">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup> Venus's atmosphere was observed in 1790 by German astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Schr%C3%B6ter" title="Johann Schröter">Johann Schröter</a>. Schröter found when the planet was a thin crescent, the cusps extended through more than 180°. He correctly surmised this was due to <a href="/wiki/Scattering" title="Scattering">scattering</a> of sunlight in a dense atmosphere. Later, American astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chester_Smith_Lyman" title="Chester Smith Lyman">Chester Smith Lyman</a> observed a complete ring around the dark side of the planet when it was at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_conjunction" title="Inferior conjunction">inferior conjunction</a>, providing further evidence for an atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> The atmosphere complicated efforts to determine a rotation period for the planet, and observers such as Italian-born astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giovanni_Cassini" title="Giovanni Cassini">Giovanni Cassini</a> and Schröter incorrectly estimated periods of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7004864000000000000♠</span>24 h</span> from the motions of markings on the planet's apparent surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground-based_research">Ground-based research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:V%C3%A9nus_t%C3%A9lescope.jpg"><img alt="black and white image of Venus, its edges blurred by its atmosphere, a small crescent of its surface illuminated" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="262" data-file-width="302" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/V%C3%A9nus_t%C3%A9lescope.jpg/220px-V%C3%A9nus_t%C3%A9lescope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/V%C3%A9nus_t%C3%A9lescope.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:V%C3%A9nus_t%C3%A9lescope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern telescopic view of Venus from Earth's surface</div></div></div>
<p>Little more was discovered about Venus until the 20th century. Its almost featureless disc gave no hint what its surface might be like, and it was only with the development of <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectroscopic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> observations that more of its secrets were revealed. The first ultraviolet observations were carried out in the 1920s, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_E._Ross" title="Frank E. Ross">Frank E. Ross</a> found that <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_photography" title="Ultraviolet photography">ultraviolet photographs</a> revealed considerable detail that was absent in visible and <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation. He suggested this was due to a dense, yellow lower atmosphere with high <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">cirrus clouds</a> above it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spectroscopic observations in the 1900s gave the first clues about the Venusian rotation. <a href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> tried to measure the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> of light from Venus, but found he could not detect any rotation. He surmised the planet must have a much longer rotation period than had previously been thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup> Later work in the 1950s showed the rotation was retrograde. <a href="/wiki/Radar_astronomy" title="Radar astronomy">Radar observations</a> of Venus were first carried out in the 1960s, and provided the first measurements of the rotation period, which were close to the modern value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Radar observations in the 1970s revealed details of the Venusian surface for the first time. Pulses of radio waves were beamed at the planet using the 300 m (980 ft) radio telescope at <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_Observatory" title="Arecibo Observatory">Arecibo Observatory</a>, and the echoes revealed two highly reflective regions, designated the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Regio" title="Alpha Regio">Alpha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_Regio" title="Beta Regio">Beta</a> regions. The observations also revealed a bright region attributed to mountains, which was called <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Montes" title="Maxwell Montes">Maxwell Montes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup> These three features are now the only ones on Venus that do not have female names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl-magellan_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpl-magellan-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Observations_and_explorations_of_Venus" title="Observations and explorations of Venus">Observations and explorations of Venus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="3029" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Mariner_2.jpg/220px-Mariner_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Mariner_2.jpg/330px-Mariner_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Mariner_2.jpg/440px-Mariner_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of <i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_2" title="Mariner 2">Mariner 2</a></i>, launched in 1962, a skeletal, bottle-shaped spacecraft with a large radio dish on top</div></div></div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> mission to Venus, and the first to any planet, began with the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Venera" title="Venera">Venera</a> program in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mitchell_1_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-mitchell_1-154">[151]</a></sup> The United States' exploration of Venus had its first success with the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_2" title="Mariner 2">Mariner 2</a> mission on 14 December 1962, becoming the world's first successful <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_spaceflight" title="Interplanetary spaceflight">interplanetary mission</a>, passing 34,833 km (21,644 mi) above the surface of Venus, and gathering data on the planet's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venera9.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="154" data-file-width="646" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Venera9.png/400px-Venera9.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Venera9.png/600px-Venera9.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/15/Venera9.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venera9.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>180-degree panorama of Venus's surface from the Soviet <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_9" title="Venera 9">Venera 9</a></i> lander, 1975. Black-and-white image of barren, black, slate-like rocks against a flat sky. The ground and the probe are the focus. Several lines are missing due to a simultaneous transmission of the scientific data</div></div></div>
<p>On 18 October 1967, the Soviet <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_4" title="Venera 4">Venera 4</a></i> successfully entered the atmosphere and deployed science experiments. <i>Venera 4</i> showed the surface temperature was hotter than <i>Mariner 2</i> had calculated, at almost 500 °C, determined that the atmosphere is 95% carbon dioxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and discovered that Venus's atmosphere was considerably denser than <i>Venera 4</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> designers had anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mitchell_2_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-mitchell_2-157">[154]</a></sup> The joint <i>Venera 4</i>–<i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_5" title="Mariner 5">Mariner 5</a></i> data were analysed by a combined Soviet–American science team in a series of colloquia over the following year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup> in an early example of space cooperation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1974, <i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a></i> swung by Venus on its way to Mercury and took ultraviolet photographs of the clouds, revealing the extraordinarily high wind speeds in the Venusian atmosphere.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="2947" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg/220px-Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg/330px-Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg/440px-Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global view of Venus in ultraviolet light done by <a href="/wiki/Mariner_10" title="Mariner 10">Mariner 10</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1975, the Soviet <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_9" title="Venera 9">Venera 9</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_10" title="Venera 10">10</a></i> landers transmitted the first images from the surface of Venus, which were in black and white. In 1982 the first colour images of the surface were obtained with the Soviet <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_13" title="Venera 13">Venera 13</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_14" title="Venera 14">14</a></i> landers.
</p><p>NASA obtained additional data in 1978 with the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_project" title="Pioneer Venus project">Pioneer Venus project</a> that consisted of two separate missions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_Orbiter" title="Pioneer Venus Orbiter">Pioneer Venus Orbiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_Multiprobe" title="Pioneer Venus Multiprobe">Pioneer Venus Multiprobe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup> The successful Soviet Venera program came to a close in October 1983, when <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_15" title="Venera 15">Venera 15</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Venera_16" title="Venera 16">16</a></i> were placed in orbit to conduct detailed mapping of 25% of Venus's terrain (from the north pole to 30°N latitude)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several other Venus flybys took place in the 1980s and 1990s that increased the understanding of Venus, including <i><a href="/wiki/Vega_1" title="Vega 1">Vega 1</a></i> (1985), <i><a href="/wiki/Vega_2" title="Vega 2">Vega 2</a></i> (1985), <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> (1990), <i><a href="/wiki/Magellan_(spacecraft)" title="Magellan (spacecraft)">Magellan</a></i> (1994), <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a></i> (1998), and <i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i> (2006). Then, <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">Venus Express</a></i> by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA) entered orbit around Venus in April 2006. Equipped with seven scientific instruments, <i>Venus Express</i> provided unprecedented long-term observation of Venus's atmosphere. ESA concluded that mission in December 2014.
</p><p>As of 2016, Japan's <i><a href="/wiki/Akatsuki_(spacecraft)" title="Akatsuki (spacecraft)">Akatsuki</a></i> is in a highly elliptical orbit around Venus since 7 December 2015, and there are <a href="/wiki/Observations_and_explorations_of_Venus#Under_study" title="Observations and explorations of Venus">several probing proposals</a> under study by <a href="/wiki/Roscosmos" title="Roscosmos">Roscosmos</a>, NASA, and India's <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">ISRO</a>.
</p><p>In 2016, NASA announced that it was planning a rover, the <a href="/wiki/Automaton_Rover_for_Extreme_Environments" title="Automaton Rover for Extreme Environments">Automaton Rover for Extreme Environments</a>, designed to survive for an extended time in Venus's environmental conditions. It would be controlled by a <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_computer" title="Mechanical computer">mechanical computer</a> and driven by wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Venus_in_fiction" title="Venus in fiction">Venus in fiction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Observations_and_explorations_of_Venus#Historical_observations_and_impact" title="Observations and explorations of Venus">Observations and explorations of Venus § Historical observations and impact</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="23756" data-file-width="30000" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Venus is portrayed just to the right of the large cypress tree in <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>'s 1889 painting <i><a href="/wiki/The_Starry_Night" title="The Starry Night">The Starry Night</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Venus is a primary feature of the night sky, and so has been of remarkable importance in <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planets_in_astrology#Venus" title="Planets in astrology">astrology</a> and fiction throughout history and in different cultures. Classical poets such as <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sappho" title="Sappho">Sappho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> spoke of the star and its light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Romantic_poetry" title="Romantic poetry">Romantic poets</a> such as <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost">Robert Frost</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred Lord Tennyson">Alfred Lord Tennyson</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>  wrote odes to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup> With the invention of the telescope, the idea that Venus was a physical world and possible destination began to take form.
</p><p>The impenetrable Venusian cloud cover gave science fiction writers free rein to speculate on conditions at its surface; all the more so when early observations showed that not only was it similar in size to Earth, it possessed a substantial atmosphere. Closer to the Sun than Earth, the planet was frequently depicted as warmer, but still <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitable</a> by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miller_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-miller-168">[165]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> reached its peak between the 1930s and 1950s, at a time when science had revealed some aspects of Venus, but not yet the harsh reality of its surface conditions. Findings from the first missions to Venus showed the reality to be quite different, and brought this particular genre to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup> As scientific knowledge of Venus advanced, so science fiction authors tried to keep pace, particularly by conjecturing human attempts to <a href="/wiki/Terraforming_of_Venus" title="Terraforming of Venus">terraform Venus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbol">Symbol</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_symbol" title="Venus symbol">Venus symbol</a></div>
<div class="floatleft"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_symbol.svg"><img alt="Venus symbol.svg" data-file-height="75" data-file-width="75" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Venus_symbol.svg/50px-Venus_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Venus_symbol.svg/75px-Venus_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Venus_symbol.svg/100px-Venus_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbols" title="Astronomical symbols">astronomical symbol</a> for Venus is the same as that used in biology for the female sex: a circle with a small cross beneath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stearn_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-stearn-171">[168]</a></sup> The Venus symbol also represents <a href="/wiki/Femininity" title="Femininity">femininity</a>, and in Western <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> stood for the metal copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stearn_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-stearn-171">[168]</a></sup> Polished copper has been used for mirrors from antiquity, and the symbol for Venus has sometimes been understood to stand for the mirror of the goddess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stearn_171-2"><a href="#cite_note-stearn-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitability">Habitability</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Venus" title="Life on Venus">Life on Venus</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Venus" title="Colonization of Venus">Colonization of Venus</a></div>
<p>The speculation of the existence of life on Venus decreased significantly since the early 1960s, when spacecraft began studying Venus and it became clear that the conditions on Venus are extreme compared to those on Earth.
</p><p>The fact that Venus is located closer to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> than <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, raising temperatures on the surface to nearly 735 K (462 °C; 863 °F), the atmospheric pressure is ninety times that of Earth, and the extreme impact of the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>, make water-based <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> as we know it unlikely. A few scientists have speculated that thermoacidophilic  <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a> might exist in the lower-temperature, acidic upper layers of the Venusian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dartnell2015_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dartnell2015-174">[171]</a></sup> The atmospheric pressure and temperature fifty kilometres above the surface are similar to those at Earth's surface. This has led to proposals to use <a href="/wiki/Aerostat" title="Aerostat">aerostats</a> (lighter-than-air balloons) for initial exploration and ultimately for permanent "floating cities" in the Venusian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landis2003_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landis2003-175">[172]</a></sup> Among the many engineering challenges are the dangerous amounts of sulfuric acid at these heights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landis2003_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landis2003-175">[172]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Several claims of transit observations made by medieval Islamic astronomers have been shown to be sunspots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VaqueroVázquez2009_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-VaqueroVázquez2009-140">[138]</a></sup> Avicenna did not record the date of his observation. There was a transit of Venus within his lifetime, on 24 May 1032, although it is questionable whether it would have been visible from his location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennard_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennard-141">[139]</a></sup></span>
